<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In mid-October 1965, the Beatles entered the recording studio; for the first time when making an album, they had an extended period without other major commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992202_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992202-134">[129]</a></sup> Until this time, according to George Martin, "we had been making albums rather like a collection of singles. Now we were really beginning to think about albums as a bit of art on their own."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> Released in December, <i><a href="/wiki/Rubber_Soul" title="Rubber Soul">Rubber Soul</a></i> has been hailed by critics as a major step forward in the maturity and complexity of the band's music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009b_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009b-133">[128]</a></sup> Their thematic reach was beginning to expand as they embraced deeper aspects of romance and philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownGaines2002181.E2.80.93182_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownGaines2002181.E2.80.93182-136">[131]</a></sup> Biographers <a href="/wiki/Peter_Brown_(music_industry)" title="Peter Brown (music industry)">Peter Brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Gaines" title="Steven Gaines">Steven Gaines</a> attribute the new musical direction to "the Beatles' now habitual use of marijuana",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownGaines2002182_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownGaines2002182-137">[132]</a></sup> an assertion confirmed by the band – Lennon referred to it as "the pot album",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000194_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000194-138">[133]</a></sup> and Starr said: "Grass was really influential in a lot of our changes, especially with the writers. And because they were writing different material, we were playing differently."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000194_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000194-138">[133]</a></sup> After <i>Help!</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s foray into the world of classical music with flutes and strings, Harrison's introduction of a <a href="/wiki/Sitar" title="Sitar">sitar</a> on "<a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Wood_(This_Bird_Has_Flown)" title="Norwegian Wood (This Bird Has Flown)">Norwegian Wood (This Bird Has Flown)</a>" marked a further progression outside the traditional boundaries of popular music. As their lyrics grew more artful, fans began to study them for deeper meaning. Of "Norwegian Wood" Lennon commented: "I was trying to be sophisticated in writing about an affair ... but in such a smokescreen way that you couldn't tell."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007297.E2.80.93298.2C_423_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007297.E2.80.93298.2C_423-139">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>While many of <i>Rubber Soul</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s prominent songs were the product of Lennon and McCartney's collaborative songwriting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005584.E2.80.93592_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005584.E2.80.93592-140">[135]</a></sup> it also featured distinct compositions from each,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997268.2C_276.2C_278.E2.80.93279_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997268.2C_276.2C_278.E2.80.93279-141">[136]</a></sup> though they continued to share official credit. The song "<a href="/wiki/In_My_Life" title="In My Life">In My Life</a>", of which each later claimed lead authorship, is considered a highlight of the entire Lennon–McCartney catalogue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005587_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005587-142">[137]</a></sup> Harrison called <i>Rubber Soul</i> his "favourite album"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000194_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000194-138">[133]</a></sup> and Starr referred to it as "the departure record".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005591_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005591-143">[138]</a></sup> McCartney has said, "We'd had our cute period, and now it was time to expand."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000197_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000197-144">[139]</a></sup> However, recording engineer <a href="/wiki/Norman_Smith_(record_producer)" title="Norman Smith (record producer)">Norman Smith</a> later stated that the studio sessions revealed signs of growing conflict within the group – "the clash between John and Paul was becoming obvious", he wrote, and "as far as Paul was concerned, George could do no right".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b780_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b780-145">[140]</a></sup> In 2003, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> ranked <i>Rubber Soul</i> fifth among "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time">The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003-146">[141]</a></sup> and AllMusic's <a href="/wiki/Richie_Unterberger" title="Richie Unterberger">Richie Unterberger</a> describes it as "one of the classic <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">folk-rock</a> records".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009a_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009a-147">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1966.E2.80.931970:_Controversy.2C_studio_years_and_break-up">1966–1970: Controversy, studio years and break-up</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Events_leading_up_to_final_tour">Events leading up to final tour</span></h4>
<p>Capitol Records, from December 1963 when it began issuing Beatles recordings for the US market, exercised complete control over format,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350-76">[76]</a></sup> compiling distinct US albums from the band's recordings and issuing songs of their choosing as singles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007295.E2.80.93296_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007295.E2.80.93296-148">[143]</a></sup> It was not until <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i> in 1967 that a Beatles album was released with identical track listings in both the UK and the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry200659_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry200659-149">[144]</a></sup> In June 1966, <i><a href="/wiki/Yesterday_and_Today" title="Yesterday and Today">Yesterday and Today</a></i>, one of Capitol's compilation albums, caused an uproar with its cover, which portrayed the grinning Beatles dressed in butcher's overalls, accompanied by raw meat and mutilated plastic baby dolls. It has been incorrectly suggested that this was meant as a satirical response to the way Capitol had "butchered" the US versions of their albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a1187_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a1187-150">[145]</a></sup> Thousands of copies of the LP had a new cover pasted over the original; an unpeeled "first-state" copy fetched $10,500 at a December 2005 auction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaffney2004_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaffney2004-151">[146]</a></sup> In England, meanwhile, Harrison met sitar maestro <a href="/wiki/Ravi_Shankar" title="Ravi Shankar">Ravi Shankar</a>, who agreed to train him on the instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavezzoli2006176_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavezzoli2006176-152">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>During a tour of the Philippines the month after the <i>Yesterday and Today</i> furore, the Beatles unintentionally snubbed the nation's first lady, <a href="/wiki/Imelda_Marcos" title="Imelda Marcos">Imelda Marcos</a>, who had expected them to attend a breakfast reception at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malaca%C3%B1an_Palace" title="Malacañan Palace">Presidential Palace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005619_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005619-153">[148]</a></sup> When presented with the invitation, Epstein politely declined on the band members' behalf, as it had never been his policy to accept such official invitations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005620_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005620-154">[149]</a></sup> They soon found that the Marcos regime was unaccustomed to taking no for an answer. The resulting riots endangered the group and they escaped the country with difficulty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005623_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005623-155">[150]</a></sup> Immediately afterwards, the band members visited India for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavezzoli2006177_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavezzoli2006177-156">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Almost as soon as they returned home, the Beatles faced a fierce backlash from US religious and social conservatives (as well as the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a>) over a comment Lennon had made in a March interview with British reporter <a href="/wiki/Maureen_Cleave" title="Maureen Cleave">Maureen Cleave</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992212.E2.80.93213_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992212.E2.80.93213-157">[152]</a></sup> "Christianity will go," Lennon had said. "It will vanish and shrink. I needn't argue about that; I'm right and I will be proved right. We're <a href="/wiki/More_popular_than_Jesus" title="More popular than Jesus">more popular than Jesus</a> now; I don't know which will go first, rock 'n' roll or Christianity. Jesus was alright but his disciples were thick and ordinary. It's them twisting it that ruins it for me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007307.E2.80.939_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007307.E2.80.939-158">[153]</a></sup> The comment went virtually unnoticed in England, but when US teenage fan magazine <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Datebook" title="Datebook">Datebook</a></i> printed it five months later – on the eve of the group's August US tour – it sparked a controversy with Christians in the American "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bible_Belt" title="Bible Belt">Bible Belt</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992212.E2.80.93213_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992212.E2.80.93213-157">[152]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican</a> issued a protest, and bans on Beatles' records were imposed by Spanish and Dutch stations and South Africa's national broadcasting service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman2008449_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman2008449-159">[154]</a></sup> Epstein accused <i>Datebook</i> of having taken Lennon's words out of context; at a press conference Lennon pointed out, "If I'd said television was more popular than Jesus, I might have got away with it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007346_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007346-160">[155]</a></sup> Lennon claimed that he was referring to how other people viewed their success, but at the prompting of reporters, he concluded: "If you want me to apologise, if that will make you happy, then okay, I'm sorry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007346_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007346-160">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>As preparations were made for the US tour, the Beatles knew that their music would hardly be heard. Having originally used <a href="/wiki/Vox_AC30" title="Vox AC30">Vox AC30</a> amplifiers, they later acquired more powerful 100-watt amplifiers, specially designed by <a href="/wiki/Vox_(musical_equipment)" title="Vox (musical equipment)">Vox</a> for them as they moved into larger venues in 1964, but these were still inadequate. Struggling to compete with the volume of sound generated by screaming fans, the band had grown increasingly bored with the routine of performing live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a1093_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a1093-161">[156]</a></sup> Recognising that their shows were no longer about the music, they decided to make the August tour their last.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992210.2C_230_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992210.2C_230-162">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolver_and_Sgt._Pepper.27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band"><i>Revolver</i> and <i>Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</i></span></h4>
<table class="mbox-small-left" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;float:left">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Beatles_eleanor_rigby.ogg" title="File:Beatles eleanor rigby.ogg">"Eleanor Rigby"</a></div>
<div>

<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "<a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Rigby" title="Eleanor Rigby">Eleanor Rigby</a>" from <i>Revolver</i> (1966). The album involves innovative compositional approaches, arrangements and recording techniques. This song, primarily written by McCartney, prominently features classical strings in a novel fusion of musical styles.</div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><i>Rubber Soul</i> had marked a major step forward; <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolver_(The_Beatles_album)" title="Revolver (The Beatles album)">Revolver</a></i>, released in August 1966 a week before the Beatles' final tour, marked another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007348_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007348-163">[158]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitchfork_Media" title="Pitchfork Media">Pitchfork</a>'s Scott Plagenhoef identifies it as "the sound of a band growing into supreme confidence" and "redefining what was expected from popular music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlagenhoef2009_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlagenhoef2009-164">[159]</a></sup> <i>Revolver</i> featured sophisticated songwriting, studio experimentation, and a greatly expanded repertoire of musical styles, ranging from innovative classical string arrangements to <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_music" title="Psychedelic music">psychedelic</a> rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007348_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007348-163">[158]</a></sup> Abandoning the customary group photograph, its cover – designed by <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Voormann" title="Klaus Voormann">Klaus Voormann</a>, a friend of the band since their Hamburg days – "was a stark, arty, black-and-white collage that caricatured the Beatles in a pen-and-ink style beholden to <a href="/wiki/Aubrey_Beardsley" title="Aubrey Beardsley">Aubrey Beardsley</a>", in Gould's description.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007348_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007348-163">[158]</a></sup> The album was preceded by the single "<a href="/wiki/Paperback_Writer" title="Paperback Writer">Paperback Writer</a>", backed by "<a href="/wiki/Rain_(Beatles_song)" title="Rain (Beatles song)">Rain</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350.E2.80.93351_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350.E2.80.93351-165">[160]</a></sup> Short promotional films were made for both songs; described by cultural historian Saul Austerlitz as "among the first true music videos",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAusterlitz200718_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAusterlitz200718-166">[161]</a></sup> they aired on <i>The Ed Sullivan Show</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops" title="Top of the Pops">Top of the Pops</a></i> in June 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992221.E2.80.93222_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992221.E2.80.93222-167">[162]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="627" data-file-width="951" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG/220px-Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG/330px-Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG/440px-Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beatles_and_George_Martin_in_studio_1966.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
At EMI Studios with George Martin in 1966</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Among the experimental songs that <i>Revolver</i> featured was "<a href="/wiki/Tomorrow_Never_Knows" title="Tomorrow Never Knows">Tomorrow Never Knows</a>", the lyrics for which Lennon drew from <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Leary" title="Timothy Leary">Timothy Leary</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Psychedelic_Experience:_A_Manual_Based_on_the_Tibetan_Book_of_the_Dead" title="The Psychedelic Experience: A Manual Based on the Tibetan Book of the Dead">The Psychedelic Experience: A Manual Based on the Tibetan Book of the Dead</a></i>. Its creation involved eight tape decks distributed about the EMI building, each staffed by an engineer or band member, who randomly varied the movement of a <a href="/wiki/Tape_loop" title="Tape loop">tape loop</a> while Martin created a composite recording by sampling the incoming data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007364.E2.80.93366_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007364.E2.80.93366-168">[163]</a></sup> McCartney's "Eleanor Rigby" made prominent use of a <a href="/wiki/Octet_(music)" title="Octet (music)">string octet</a>; Gould describes it as "a true hybrid, conforming to no recognisable style or genre of song".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007350.2C_402_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007350.2C_402-169">[164]</a></sup> Harrison was developing as a songwriter, and three of his compositions earned a place on the record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992224_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992224-170">[165]</a></sup> In 2003, <i>Rolling Stone</i> ranked <i>Revolver</i> as the third greatest album of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003-146">[141]</a></sup> During the US tour that followed its release, however, the band performed none of its songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992361.E2.80.93365_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992361.E2.80.93365-171">[166]</a></sup> As Chris Ingham writes, they were very much "studio creations ... and there was no way a four-piece rock 'n' roll group could do them justice, particularly through the desensitising wall of the fans' screams. 'Live Beatles' and 'Studio Beatles' had become entirely different beasts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200644_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200644-172">[167]</a></sup> The band's final concert at San Francisco's <a href="/wiki/Candlestick_Park" title="Candlestick Park">Candlestick Park</a> on 29 August was their last commercial concert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997293.E2.80.93295_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997293.E2.80.93295-173">[168]</a></sup> It marked the end of a four-year period dominated by almost nonstop touring that included over 1,400 concert appearances internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould20075.E2.80.936.2C_249.2C_281.2C_347_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould20075.E2.80.936.2C_249.2C_281.2C_347-174">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Freed from the burden of touring, the Beatles embraced an increasingly experimental approach as they recorded <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i>, beginning in late November 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992232_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992232-175">[170]</a></sup> According to engineer <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Emerick" title="Geoff Emerick">Geoff Emerick</a>, the album's recording took over 700 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006190_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006190-176">[171]</a></sup> He recalled the band's insistence "that everything on <i>Sgt. Pepper</i> had to be different. We had microphones right down in the bells of brass instruments and headphones turned into microphones attached to violins. We used giant primitive oscillators to vary the speed of instruments and vocals and we had tapes chopped to pieces and stuck together upside down and the wrong way around."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007387.E2.80.93388_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007387.E2.80.93388-177">[172]</a></sup> Parts of "<a href="/wiki/A_Day_in_the_Life" title="A Day in the Life">A Day in the Life</a>" featured a 40-piece orchestra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007387.E2.80.93388_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007387.E2.80.93388-177">[172]</a></sup> The sessions initially yielded the non-album <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_A-side" title="Double A-side">double A-side</a> single "<a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Fields_Forever" title="Strawberry Fields Forever">Strawberry Fields Forever</a>"/"<a href="/wiki/Penny_Lane" title="Penny Lane">Penny Lane</a>" in February 1967;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005221_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005221-178">[173]</a></sup> the <i>Sgt. Pepper</i> LP followed in June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005220_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005220-179">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>The musical complexity of the records, created using relatively primitive <a href="/wiki/Multitrack_recording" title="Multitrack recording">four-track</a> recording technology, astounded contemporary artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a970_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a970-180">[175]</a></sup> Among music critics, acclaim for the album was virtually universal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007420.E2.80.93425_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007420.E2.80.93425-181">[176]</a></sup> Gould writes:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The overwhelming consensus is that the Beatles had created a popular masterpiece: a rich, sustained, and overflowing work of collaborative genius whose bold ambition and startling originality dramatically enlarged the possibilities and raised the expectations of what the experience of listening to popular music on record could be. On the basis of this perception, <i>Sgt. Pepper</i> became the catalyst for an explosion of mass enthusiasm for album-formatted rock that would revolutionise both the aesthetics and the economics of the record business in ways that far outstripped the earlier pop explosions triggered by the Elvis phenomenon of 1956 and the Beatlemania phenomenon of 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007418_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007418-182">[177]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band.jpg/220px-Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/50/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Front cover of <i>Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</i>, "the most famous cover of any music album, and one of the most imitated images in the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a970_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a970-180">[175]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Sgt. Pepper</i> was the first major pop/rock LP to include its complete lyrics, which appeared on the back cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992236_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992236-183">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInglis200896_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInglis200896-184">[179]</a></sup> Those lyrics were the subject of critical analysis; for instance, in late 1967 the album was the subject of a scholarly inquiry by American literary critic and professor of English <a href="/wiki/Richard_Poirier" title="Richard Poirier">Richard Poirier</a>, who observed that his students were "listening to the group's music with a degree of engagement that he, as a teacher of literature, could only envy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007423.E2.80.93425_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007423.E2.80.93425-185">[180]</a></sup> Poirier identified what he termed its "mixed allusiveness": "It's unwise ever to assume that they're doing only one thing or expressing themselves in only one style ... one kind of feeling about a subject isn't enough ... any single induced feeling must often exist within the context of seemingly contradictory alternatives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007423.E2.80.93425_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007423.E2.80.93425-185">[180]</a></sup> McCartney said at the time: "We write songs. We know what we mean by them. But in a week someone else says something about it, and you can't deny it. ... You put your own meaning at your own level to our songs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007423.E2.80.93425_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007423.E2.80.93425-185">[180]</a></sup> In 2003, <i>Rolling Stone</i> ranked it number one on its list of the "500 Greatest Albums of All Time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003-146">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Sgt. Pepper</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s elaborate cover also attracted considerable interest and study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007394.E2.80.9395_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007394.E2.80.9395-186">[181]</a></sup> A collage designed by <a href="/wiki/Pop_art" title="Pop art">pop artists</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Blake_(artist)" title="Peter Blake (artist)">Peter Blake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jann_Haworth" title="Jann Haworth">Jann Haworth</a>, it depicted the group as the fictional band referred to in the album's <a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band_(song)" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band (song)">title track</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005312_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005312-187">[182]</a></sup> standing in front of <a href="/wiki/List_of_images_on_the_cover_of_Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="List of images on the cover of Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band">a crowd of famous people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000248_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000248-188">[183]</a></sup> The heavy moustaches worn by the group reflected the growing influence of <a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippie</a> style,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000236_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000236-189">[184]</a></sup> while cultural historian Jonathan Harris describes their "brightly coloured parodies of military uniforms" as a knowingly "anti-authoritarian and anti-establishment" display.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris200512.E2.80.9313_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris200512.E2.80.9313-190">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 June 1967, the Beatles performed their forthcoming single, "<a href="/wiki/All_You_Need_Is_Love" title="All You Need Is Love">All You Need Is Love</a>", to an estimated 350 million viewers on <i><a href="/wiki/Our_World_(TV_special)" title="Our World (TV special)">Our World</a></i>, the first live global television link.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992237.2C_259.E2.80.93260_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992237.2C_259.E2.80.93260-191">[186]</a></sup> Released a week later, during the <a href="/wiki/Summer_of_Love" title="Summer of Love">Summer of Love</a>, the song was adopted as a <a href="/wiki/Flower_power" title="Flower power">flower power</a> anthem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007428.E2.80.93429_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007428.E2.80.93429-192">[187]</a></sup> Two months later, the group suffered a loss that threw their career into turmoil. Having been introduced to <a href="/wiki/Maharishi_Mahesh_Yogi" title="Maharishi Mahesh Yogi">Maharishi Mahesh Yogi</a> only the previous night in London, on 25 August they travelled to <a href="/wiki/Bangor,_Gwynedd" title="Bangor, Gwynedd">Bangor</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation" title="Transcendental Meditation">Transcendental Meditation</a> retreat.</p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Brian Epstein was dead ... Paul and George were in complete shock. I don't think it could have been worse if they had heard that their own fathers had dropped dead … Brian had found them, believed in them, molded them, turned them into millionaires, and made them famous the world over … We knew that life would never be the same again.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">– <a href="/wiki/Pattie_Boyd" title="Pattie Boyd">Pattie Boyd</a>, wife of George Harrison<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd2008106-107_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd2008106-107-193">[188]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Two days later, their manager's assistant, Peter Brown, phoned to inform them that Epstein, only thirty-two years old, had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005709.2C_713.E2.80.93719_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005709.2C_713.E2.80.93719-194">[189]</a></sup> The coroner ruled the death an accidental <a href="/wiki/Bromoureide" title="Bromoureide">carbitol</a> overdose, although it was widely rumoured to be a suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownGaines2002249_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownGaines2002249-195">[190]</a></sup> Epstein had been in a fragile emotional state, stressed by personal issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[nb 6]</a></sup> His death left the group disoriented and fearful about the future. Lennon recalled: "We collapsed. I knew that we were in trouble then. I didn't really have any misconceptions about our ability to do anything other than play music, and I was scared. I thought, 'We've had it now.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000268_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000268-198">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magical_Mystery_Tour.2C_the_White_Album_and_Yellow_Submarine"><i>Magical Mystery Tour</i>, the White Album and <i>Yellow Submarine</i></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1963" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg/220px-The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg/330px-The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg/440px-The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Beatles_magical_mystery_tour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Filming a sequence for "<a href="/wiki/I_Am_the_Walrus" title="I Am the Walrus">I Am the Walrus</a>"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Magical_Mystery_Tour" title="Magical Mystery Tour">Magical Mystery Tour</a></i>, the soundtrack to a forthcoming Beatles television film, was released in the UK as a six-track double <a href="/wiki/Extended_play" title="Extended play">extended play disc (EP)</a> in early December 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007452_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007452-199">[193]</a></sup> In the United States, the six songs were issued on an identically titled LP that also included five tracks from the band's recent singles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992351_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992351-99">[97]</a></sup> Unterberger says of the US <i>Magical Mystery Tour</i>, "the psychedelic sound is very much in the vein of <i>Sgt. Pepper</i>, and even spacier in parts (especially the sound collages of '<a href="/wiki/I_Am_the_Walrus" title="I Am the Walrus">I Am the Walrus</a>')" and he calls its five songs culled from the band's 1967 singles "huge, glorious, and innovative".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009c_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009c-200">[194]</a></sup> In its first three weeks, the album set a record for the highest initial sales of any Capitol LP, and it is the only Capitol compilation later to be adopted in the band's official canon of studio albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a699_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a699-201">[195]</a></sup> First aired on <a href="/wiki/Boxing_Day" title="Boxing Day">Boxing Day</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Magical_Mystery_Tour_(film)" title="Magical Mystery Tour (film)"><i>Magical Mystery Tour</i></a> film, largely directed by McCartney, brought the group their first major negative UK press. It was dismissed as "blatant rubbish" by the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i>; the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> called it "a colossal conceit"; and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> labelled the film "a kind of fantasy morality play about the grossness and warmth and stupidity of the audience".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007455.E2.80.93456_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007455.E2.80.93456-202">[196]</a></sup> Gould describes it as "a great deal of raw footage showing a group of people getting on, getting off, and riding on a bus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007455.E2.80.93456_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007455.E2.80.93456-202">[196]</a></sup> Although the viewership figures were respectable, its slating in the press led US television networks to lose interest in broadcasting the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a703_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a703-203">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 1968, the Beatles filmed a cameo for the animated movie <i><a href="/wiki/Yellow_Submarine_(film)" title="Yellow Submarine (film)">Yellow Submarine</a></i>, which featured cartoon versions of the band members and a soundtrack with eleven of their songs, including four unreleased studio recordings that made their debut in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007485_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007485-204">[198]</a></sup> Released in June 1968, the film was praised by critics for its music, humour and innovative visual style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007487.2C_505.E2.80.93506_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007487.2C_505.E2.80.93506-205">[199]</a></sup> It would be seven months, however, before the film's soundtrack album appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992304.2C_350_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992304.2C_350-206">[200]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheBeatles68LP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="720" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/TheBeatles68LP.jpg/220px-TheBeatles68LP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/TheBeatles68LP.jpg/330px-TheBeatles68LP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/TheBeatles68LP.jpg/440px-TheBeatles68LP.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheBeatles68LP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Beatles</i>, known as the White Album for its minimalist cover, conceived by pop artist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hamilton_(artist)" title="Richard Hamilton (artist)">Richard Hamilton</a> "in direct contrast to <i>Sgt. Pepper</i>", while also suggesting a "clean slate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007510.E2.80.93511_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007510.E2.80.93511-207">[201]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the interim came <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_(album)" title="The Beatles (album)">The Beatles</a></i>, a double LP commonly known as the White Album for its virtually featureless cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007510_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007510-208">[202]</a></sup> Creative inspiration for the album came from a new direction: without Epstein's guiding presence, the group had briefly turned to Maharishi Mahesh Yogi as their <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a705.E2.80.93706_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a705.E2.80.93706-209">[203]</a></sup> At his <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashram</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rishikesh" title="Rishikesh">Rishikesh</a>, India, a "Guide Course" scheduled for three months marked <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_in_India" title="The Beatles in India">one of their most prolific periods</a>, yielding numerous songs including a majority of the 30 included on the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a108.E2.80.93109_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a108.E2.80.93109-210">[204]</a></sup> However, Starr left after only ten days, likening it to <a href="/wiki/Butlins" title="Butlins">Butlins</a>, and McCartney eventually grew bored and departed a month later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007463.E2.80.93468_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007463.E2.80.93468-211">[205]</a></sup> For Lennon and Harrison, creativity turned to questioning when an electronics technician known as <a href="/wiki/Magic_Alex" title="Magic Alex">Magic Alex</a> suggested that the Maharishi was attempting to manipulate them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a705.E2.80.93706_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a705.E2.80.93706-209">[203]</a></sup> When he alleged that the Maharishi had made sexual advances to women attendees, a persuaded Lennon left abruptly just two months into the course, bringing an unconvinced Harrison and the remainder of the group's entourage with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007463.E2.80.93468_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007463.E2.80.93468-211">[205]</a></sup> In anger, Lennon wrote a scathing song titled "Maharishi", renamed "<a href="/wiki/Sexy_Sadie" title="Sexy Sadie">Sexy Sadie</a>" to avoid potential legal issues. McCartney said, "We made a mistake. We thought there was more to him than there was."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a705.E2.80.93706_209-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a705.E2.80.93706-209">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>During recording sessions for the White Album, which stretched from late May to mid-October 1968, relations between the Beatles grew openly divisive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992283.E2.80.93304_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992283.E2.80.93304-212">[206]</a></sup> Starr quit for two weeks, and McCartney took over the drum kit for "<a href="/wiki/Back_in_the_U.S.S.R." title="Back in the U.S.S.R.">Back in the U.S.S.R.</a>" (on which Harrison and Lennon drummed as well) and "<a href="/wiki/Dear_Prudence" title="Dear Prudence">Dear Prudence</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinn2009205.E2.80.93207_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinn2009205.E2.80.93207-213">[207]</a></sup> Lennon had lost interest in collaborating with McCartney,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007513.2C_516_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007513.2C_516-214">[208]</a></sup> whose contribution "<a href="/wiki/Ob-La-Di,_Ob-La-Da" title="Ob-La-Di, Ob-La-Da">Ob-La-Di, Ob-La-Da</a>" he scorned as "granny music shit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006246_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006246-215">[209]</a></sup> Tensions were further aggravated by Lennon's romantic preoccupation with <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">Yoko Ono</a>, whom he insisted on bringing to the sessions despite the group's well-established understanding that girlfriends were not allowed in the studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b103_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b103-216">[210]</a></sup> Describing the double album, Lennon later said: "Every track is an individual track; there isn't any Beatle music on it. [It's] John and the band, Paul and the band, George and the band."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007509_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007509-217">[211]</a></sup> McCartney has recalled that the album "wasn't a pleasant one to make".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000310_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000310-218">[212]</a></sup> Both he and Lennon identified the sessions as the start of the band's break-up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000237_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000237-219">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b102_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b102-220">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>Issued in November, the White Album was the band's first <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">Apple Records</a> album release, although EMI continued to own their recordings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992278_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992278-221">[215]</a></sup> The new label was a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Apple_Corps" title="Apple Corps">Apple Corps</a>, which Epstein had formed as part of his plan to create a tax-effective business structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007470_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007470-222">[216]</a></sup> The record attracted more than 2 million advance orders, selling nearly 4 million copies in the US in little over a month, and its tracks dominated the playlists of American radio stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007528_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007528-223">[217]</a></sup> Despite its popularity, it did not receive flattering reviews at the time. According to Gould:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The critical response ... ranged from mixed to flat. In marked contrast to <i>Sgt. Pepper</i>, which had helped to establish an entire genre of literate rock criticism, the White Album inspired no critical writing of any note. Even the most sympathetic reviewers ... clearly didn't know what to make of this shapeless outpouring of songs. <i>Newsweek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Hubert Saal, citing the high proportion of parodies, accused the group of getting their tongues caught in their cheeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007528_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007528-223">[217]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>General critical opinion eventually turned in favour of the White Album, and in 2003, <i>Rolling Stone</i> ranked it as the tenth greatest album of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003_146-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERolling_Stone2003-146">[141]</a></sup> Pitchfork's Mark Richardson describes it as "large and sprawling, overflowing with ideas but also with indulgences, and filled with a hugely variable array of material ... its failings are as essential to its character as its triumphs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichardson2009_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichardson2009-224">[218]</a></sup> Erlewine comments: "The [band's] two main songwriting forces were no longer on the same page, but neither were George and Ringo", yet "Lennon turns in two of his best ballads", McCartney's songs are "stunning", Harrison had become "a songwriter who deserved wider exposure", and Starr's composition was "a delight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEErlewine2009d_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEErlewine2009d-225">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Yellow_Submarine_(album)" title="Yellow Submarine (album)">Yellow Submarine</a></i> LP, issued in January 1969, contained only the four previously unreleased songs that had debuted in the film, along with the title track (already issued on <i>Revolver</i>), "All You Need Is Love" (already issued as a single and on the US <i>Magical Mystery Tour</i> LP) and seven instrumental pieces composed by Martin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992304.2C_350_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992304.2C_350-206">[200]</a></sup> Because of the paucity of new Beatles music, AllMusic's Unterberger and Bruce Eder suggest the album might be "inessential" but for Harrison's "<a href="/wiki/It%27s_All_Too_Much" title="It's All Too Much">It's All Too Much</a>": "the jewel of the new songs ... resplendent in swirling <a href="/wiki/Mellotron" title="Mellotron">Mellotron</a>, larger-than-life percussion, and tidal waves of feedback guitar ... a virtuoso excursion into otherwise hazy psychedelia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterbergerEder2009_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterbergerEder2009-226">[220]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:right;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbey_Road.2C_Let_It_Be.2C_and_break-up"><i>Abbey Road</i>, <i>Let It Be</i>, and break-up</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2314" data-file-width="3442" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg/220px-Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg/330px-Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg/440px-Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Billy_Preston_perforning_in_1971.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
American soul musician Billy Preston (pictured in 1971) was for a short time considered practically a <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Beatle" title="Fifth Beatle">fifth Beatle</a> during the recording of <i>Get Back</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although <i><a href="/wiki/Let_It_Be" title="Let It Be">Let It Be</a></i> was the Beatles' final album release, it was largely recorded before <i><a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road" title="Abbey Road">Abbey Road</a></i>. The project's impetus came from an idea Martin attributes to McCartney, who suggested they "record an album of new material and rehearse it, then perform it before a live audience for the very first time – on record and on film".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539-227">[221]</a></sup> Originally intended for a one-hour television programme to be called <i>Beatles at Work</i>, much of the album's content came from extensive rehearsals filmed by director <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lindsay-Hogg" title="Michael Lindsay-Hogg">Michael Lindsay-Hogg</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twickenham_Film_Studios" title="Twickenham Film Studios">Twickenham Film Studios</a>, beginning in January 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539-227">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992306.E2.80.93307_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992306.E2.80.93307-228">[222]</a></sup> Martin has said that the project was "not at all a happy recording experience. It was a time when relations between the Beatles were at their lowest ebb."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539_227-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539-227">[221]</a></sup> Lennon described the largely impromptu sessions as "hell ... the most miserable ... on Earth", and Harrison, "the low of all-time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992310_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992310-229">[223]</a></sup> Irritated by both McCartney and Lennon, Harrison walked out for five days. Upon returning, he threatened to leave the band unless they "abandon[ed] all talk of live performance" and instead focused on finishing a new album, initially titled <i>Get Back</i>, using songs recorded for the TV special.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992307_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992307-230">[224]</a></sup> He also demanded they cease work at Twickenham and relocate to the newly finished <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_Studio" title="Apple Studio">Apple Studio</a>. The other band members agreed, and the idea came about to salvage the footage shot for the TV production for use in a feature film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992306.E2.80.93307.2C_309_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992306.E2.80.93307.2C_309-231">[225]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3_Savile_Row.jpg"><img alt="A terrace house with four floors and an attic. It is red brick, with a slate roof, and the ground floor rendered in imitation of stone and painted white. Each upper floor has four sash windows, divided into small panes. The door, with a canopy over it, occupies the place of the second window from the left on the ground floor." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="2592" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/3_Savile_Row.jpg/170px-3_Savile_Row.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/3_Savile_Row.jpg/255px-3_Savile_Row.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/3_Savile_Row.jpg/340px-3_Savile_Row.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3_Savile_Row.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apple Corps building at 3 Savile Row, site of the <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles%27_rooftop_concert" title="The Beatles' rooftop concert"><i>Let It Be</i> rooftop concert</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In an effort to alleviate tensions within the band and improve the quality of their live sound, Harrison invited keyboardist <a href="/wiki/Billy_Preston" title="Billy Preston">Billy Preston</a> to participate in the last nine days of sessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992309.E2.80.93314_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992309.E2.80.93314-232">[226]</a></sup> Preston received label billing on the "<a href="/wiki/Get_Back" title="Get Back">Get Back</a>" single – the only musician ever to receive that acknowledgment on an official Beatles release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a451.2C_660_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a451.2C_660-233">[227]</a></sup> At the conclusion of the rehearsals, the band could not agree on a location to film a concert, rejecting several ideas, including a boat at sea, a lunatic asylum, the Tunisian desert, and the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539_227-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000b539-227">[221]</a></sup> Ultimately, what would be their <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles%27_rooftop_concert" title="The Beatles' rooftop concert">final live performance</a> was filmed on the rooftop of the Apple Corps building at 3 <a href="/wiki/Savile_Row" title="Savile Row">Savile Row</a>, London, on 30 January 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992307.E2.80.93308.2C_312_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992307.E2.80.93308.2C_312-234">[228]</a></sup> Five weeks later, engineer <a href="/wiki/Glyn_Johns" title="Glyn Johns">Glyn Johns</a>, whom Lewisohn describes as <i>Get Back</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s "uncredited producer", began work assembling an album, given "free rein" as the band "all but washed their hands of the entire project".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992309.2C_316.E2.80.93323_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992309.2C_316.E2.80.93323-235">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>New strains developed between the band members regarding the appointment of a financial adviser, the need for which had become evident without Epstein to manage business affairs. Lennon, Harrison and Starr favoured <a href="/wiki/Allen_Klein" title="Allen Klein">Allen Klein</a>, who had managed <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Cooke" title="Sam Cooke">Sam Cooke</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a612_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a612-236">[230]</a></sup> McCartney wanted <a href="/wiki/Lee_Eastman" title="Lee Eastman">Lee</a> and John Eastman – father and brother, respectively, of <a href="/wiki/Linda_McCartney" title="Linda McCartney">Linda Eastman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201170.2C_132_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201170.2C_132-237">[231]</a></sup> whom McCartney married on 12 March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001336_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001336-238">[232]</a></sup> Agreement could not be reached, so both Klein and the Eastmans were temporarily appointed: Klein as the Beatles' business manager and the Eastmans as their lawyers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201171.E2.80.9372_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201171.E2.80.9372-239">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodman2015164.E2.80.93166_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodman2015164.E2.80.93166-240">[234]</a></sup> Further conflict ensued, however, and financial opportunities were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a612_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a612-236">[230]</a></sup> On 8 May, Klein was named sole manager of the band,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992322_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992322-241">[235]</a></sup> the Eastmans having previously been dismissed as the Beatles' attorneys. McCartney refused to sign the management contract with Klein, but he was out-voted by the other Beatles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodman2015174.E2.80.93175_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodman2015174.E2.80.93175-242">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>Martin stated that he was surprised when McCartney asked him to produce another album, as the <i>Get Back</i> sessions had been "a miserable experience" and he had "thought it was the end of the road for all of us".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007560_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007560-243">[237]</a></sup> The primary recording sessions for <i>Abbey Road</i> began on 2 July 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992324_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992324-244">[238]</a></sup> Lennon, who rejected Martin's proposed format of a "continuously moving piece of music", wanted his and McCartney's songs to occupy separate sides of the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007563_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007563-245">[239]</a></sup> The eventual format, with individually composed songs on the first side and the second consisting largely of a <a href="/wiki/List_of_musical_medleys" title="List of musical medleys">medley</a>, was McCartney's suggested compromise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007563_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007563-245">[239]</a></sup> On 4 July, the first solo single by a Beatle was released: Lennon's "<a href="/wiki/Give_Peace_a_Chance" title="Give Peace a Chance">Give Peace a Chance</a>", credited to the <a href="/wiki/Plastic_Ono_Band" title="Plastic Ono Band">Plastic Ono Band</a>. The completion and mixing of "<a href="/wiki/I_Want_You_(She%27s_So_Heavy)" title="I Want You (She's So Heavy)">I Want You (She's So Heavy)</a>" on 20 August 1969 was the last occasion on which all four Beatles were together in the same studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988191_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988191-246">[240]</a></sup> Lennon announced his departure to the rest of the group on 20 September, but agreed to withhold a public announcement to avoid undermining sales of the forthcoming album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman2008622.E2.80.93624_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman2008622.E2.80.93624-247">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>Released six days after Lennon's declaration, <i>Abbey Road</i> sold 4 million copies within three months and topped the UK charts for a total of seventeen weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007593_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007593-248">[242]</a></sup> Its second track, the ballad "<a href="/wiki/Something_(Beatles_song)" title="Something (Beatles song)">Something</a>", was issued as a single – the only Harrison composition ever to appear as a Beatles A-side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997553_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997553-249">[243]</a></sup> <i>Abbey Road</i> received mixed reviews, although the medley met with general acclaim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007593_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007593-248">[242]</a></sup> Unterberger considers it "a fitting swan song for the group", containing "some of the greatest harmonies to be heard on any rock record".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009d_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009d-250">[244]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Musicology" title="Musicology">Musicologist</a> and author <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacDonald" title="Ian MacDonald">Ian MacDonald</a> calls the album "erratic and often hollow", despite the "semblance of unity and coherence" offered by the medley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005367_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005367-251">[245]</a></sup> Martin has singled it out as his personal favourite of all the band's albums; Lennon said it was "competent" but had "no life in it". Recording engineer Emerick notes that the replacement of the studio's <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">valve</a> mixing console with a transistorised one yielded a less punchy sound, leaving the group frustrated at the thinner tone and lack of impact and contributing to its "kinder, gentler" feel relative to their previous albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006277.E2.80.93278_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006277.E2.80.93278-252">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the still unfinished <i>Get Back</i> album, one last song, Harrison's "<a href="/wiki/I_Me_Mine" title="I Me Mine">I Me Mine</a>", was recorded on 3 January 1970. Lennon, in Denmark at the time, did not participate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992342_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992342-253">[247]</a></sup> In March, rejecting the work Johns had done on the project, now retitled <i>Let It Be</i>, Klein gave the session tapes to American producer <a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">Phil Spector</a>, who had recently produced Lennon's solo single "<a href="/wiki/Instant_Karma!" title="Instant Karma!">Instant Karma!</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992342.E2.80.93343_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992342.E2.80.93343-254">[248]</a></sup> In addition to remixing the material, Spector edited, spliced and overdubbed several of the recordings that had been intended as "live". McCartney was unhappy with the producer's approach and particularly dissatisfied with the lavish orchestration on "<a href="/wiki/The_Long_and_Winding_Road" title="The Long and Winding Road">The Long and Winding Road</a>", which involved a fourteen-voice choir and 36-piece instrumental ensemble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992349_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992349-255">[249]</a></sup> McCartney's demands that the alterations to the song be reverted were ignored,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a682_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a682-256">[250]</a></sup> and he publicly announced his departure from the band on 10 April 1970, a week before the release of his first, <a href="/wiki/McCartney_(album)" title="McCartney (album)">self-titled solo album</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992349_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992349-255">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005853_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005853-257">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 8 May, the Spector-produced <i>Let It Be</i> was released. Its accompanying single, "The Long and Winding Road", was the Beatles' last; it was released in the United States, but not in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350.E2.80.93351_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1992350.E2.80.93351-165">[160]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Let_It_Be_(film)" title="Let It Be (film)">Let It Be</a></i> documentary film followed later that month, and would win the 1970 Academy Award for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Music_(Scoring)#1970s" title="Academy Award for Best Music (Scoring)">Best Original Song Score</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry200696_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry200696-258">[252]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunday_Telegraph" title="Sunday Telegraph">Sunday Telegraph</a></i> critic <a href="/wiki/Penelope_Gilliatt" title="Penelope Gilliatt">Penelope Gilliatt</a> called it "a very bad film and a touching one ... about the breaking apart of this reassuring, geometrically perfect, once apparently ageless family of siblings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007600_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007600-259">[253]</a></sup> Several reviewers stated that some of the performances in the film sounded better than their analogous album tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007601_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007601-260">[254]</a></sup> Describing <i>Let It Be</i> as the "only Beatles album to occasion negative, even hostile reviews", Unterberger calls it "on the whole underrated"; he singles out "some good moments of straight hard rock in '<a href="/wiki/I%27ve_Got_a_Feeling" title="I've Got a Feeling">I've Got a Feeling</a>' and '<a href="/wiki/Dig_a_Pony" title="Dig a Pony">Dig a Pony</a>'", and praises "<a href="/wiki/Let_It_Be_(song)" title="Let It Be (song)">Let It Be</a>", "Get Back", and "the folky '<a href="/wiki/Two_of_Us_(song)" title="Two of Us (song)">Two of Us</a>', with John and Paul harmonising together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009e_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterberger2009e-261">[255]</a></sup> McCartney filed suit for the dissolution of the Beatles' contractual partnership on 31 December 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2002139_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2002139-262">[256]</a></sup> Legal disputes continued long after their break-up, and the dissolution was not formalised until 29 December 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2002150_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2002150-263">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970.E2.80.93present:_After_the_break-up">1970–present: After the break-up</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Collaborations_between_ex-Beatles" title="Collaborations between ex-Beatles">Collaborations between ex-Beatles</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span></h4>
<p>Lennon, McCartney, Harrison and Starr all released solo albums in 1970. Their solo records sometimes involved one or more of the others;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007601.E2.80.93604_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007601.E2.80.93604-264">[258]</a></sup> Starr's <i><a href="/wiki/Ringo_(album)" title="Ringo (album)">Ringo</a></i> (1973) was the only album to include compositions and performances by all four ex-Beatles, albeit on separate songs. With Starr's participation, Harrison staged <a href="/wiki/The_Concert_for_Bangladesh" title="The Concert for Bangladesh">the Concert for Bangladesh</a> in New York City in August 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2007603.E2.80.93604_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2007603.E2.80.93604-265">[259]</a></sup> Other than an unreleased jam session in 1974, later <a href="/wiki/Bootleg_recording" title="Bootleg recording">bootlegged</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/A_Toot_and_a_Snore_in_%2774" title="A Toot and a Snore in '74">A Toot and a Snore in '74</a></i>, Lennon and McCartney never recorded together again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandford2006227.E2.80.93229_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandford2006227.E2.80.93229-266">[260]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two double-LP sets of the Beatles' greatest hits, compiled by Klein, <i><a href="/wiki/1962%E2%80%931966" title="1962–1966">1962–1966</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/1967%E2%80%931970" title="1967–1970">1967–1970</a></i>, were released in 1973, at first under the Apple Records imprint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200669_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200669-267">[261]</a></sup> Commonly known as the <i>Red Album</i> and <i>Blue Album</i>, respectively, each have earned a <a href="/wiki/Music_recording_sales_certification" title="Music recording sales certification">Multi-Platinum certification</a> in the United States and a Platinum certification in the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERIAA2009b_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERIAA2009b-268">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBritish_Phonographic_Industry2009_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBritish_Phonographic_Industry2009-269">[263]</a></sup> Between 1976 and 1982, EMI/Capitol released a wave of compilation albums without input from the ex-Beatles, starting with the double-disc compilation <i><a href="/wiki/Rock_%27n%27_Roll_Music_(album)" title="Rock 'n' Roll Music (album)">Rock 'n' Roll Music</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry2006109_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry2006109-270">[264]</a></sup> The only one to feature previously unreleased material was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_at_the_Hollywood_Bowl" title="The Beatles at the Hollywood Bowl">The Beatles at the Hollywood Bowl</a></i> (1977); the first officially issued concert recordings by the group, it contained selections from two shows they played during their 1964 and 1965 US tours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.2C_69_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.2C_69-271">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[nb 7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The music and enduring fame of the Beatles has been commercially exploited in various other ways, again often outside their creative control. In April 1974, the musical <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John,_Paul,_George,_Ringo_%E2%80%A6_and_Bert" title="John, Paul, George, Ringo … and Bert">John, Paul, George, Ringo … and Bert</a></i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Willy_Russell" title="Willy Russell">Willy Russell</a> and featuring singer <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Dickson" title="Barbara Dickson">Barbara Dickson</a>, opened in London. It included, with permission from Northern Songs, eleven Lennon-McCartney compositions and one by Harrison, "<a href="/wiki/Here_Comes_the_Sun" title="Here Comes the Sun">Here Comes the Sun</a>". Displeased with the production's use of his song, Harrison withdrew his permission to use it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry2006109.E2.80.93110_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthallPerry2006109.E2.80.93110-274">[267]</a></sup> Later that year, the off-Broadway musical <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band_on_the_Road" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band on the Road">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band on the Road</a></i> opened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[268]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/All_This_and_World_War_II" title="All This and World War II">All This and World War II</a></i> (1976) was an unorthodox nonfiction film that combined newsreel footage with covers of Beatles songs by performers ranging from <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Moon" title="Keith Moon">Keith Moon</a> to the <a href="/wiki/London_Symphony_Orchestra" title="London Symphony Orchestra">London Symphony Orchestra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodriguez2010306.E2.80.93307_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodriguez2010306.E2.80.93307-276">[269]</a></sup> The Broadway musical <i><a href="/wiki/Beatlemania_(musical)" title="Beatlemania (musical)">Beatlemania</a></i>, an unauthorised nostalgia revue, opened in early 1977 and proved popular, spinning off five separate touring productions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.E2.80.9367_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.E2.80.9367-277">[270]</a></sup> In 1979, the band sued the producers, settling for several million dollars in damages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.E2.80.9367_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.E2.80.9367-277">[270]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band_(film)" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band (film)">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i> (1978), a musical film starring the <a href="/wiki/Bee_Gees" title="Bee Gees">Bee Gees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Frampton" title="Peter Frampton">Peter Frampton</a>, was a commercial failure and an "artistic fiasco", according to Ingham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200666_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200666-278">[271]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span></h4>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_John_Lennon" title="Death of John Lennon">murder of Lennon</a> in December 1980, Harrison rewrote the lyrics to his song "<a href="/wiki/All_Those_Years_Ago" title="All Those Years Ago">All Those Years Ago</a>" in Lennon's honour. With Starr on drums and McCartney and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Linda_McCartney" title="Linda McCartney">Linda</a>, contributing backing vocals, the song was released as a single in May 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman1999284_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman1999284-279">[272]</a></sup> McCartney's own tribute, "<a href="/wiki/Here_Today_(Paul_McCartney_song)" title="Here Today (Paul McCartney song)">Here Today</a>", appeared on his <i><a href="/wiki/Tug_of_War_(Paul_McCartney_album)" title="Tug of War (Paul McCartney album)">Tug of War</a></i> album in April 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2002412.E2.80.93413_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2002412.E2.80.93413-280">[273]</a></sup> In 1987, Harrison's <i><a href="/wiki/Cloud_Nine_(George_Harrison_album)" title="Cloud Nine (George Harrison album)">Cloud Nine</a></i> album included "<a href="/wiki/When_We_Was_Fab" title="When We Was Fab">When We Was Fab</a>", a song about the Beatlemania era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett2009292_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett2009292-281">[274]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the Beatles' studio albums were released on CD by EMI and Apple Corps in 1987, their catalogue was standardised throughout the world, establishing a canon of the twelve original studio LPs as issued in the UK plus the US LP version of <i>Magical Mystery Tour</i> (1967).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI7_April_2009_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI7_April_2009-282">[275]</a></sup> All the remaining material from the singles and EPs which had not appeared on the original studio albums was gathered on the two-volume compilation <i><a href="/wiki/Past_Masters" title="Past Masters">Past Masters</a></i> (1988). Except for the <i>Red</i> and <i>Blue</i> albums, EMI deleted all its other Beatles compilations – including the <i>Hollywood Bowl</i> record – from its catalogue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.2C_69_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIngham200666.2C_69-271">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1988, the Beatles were inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a>, their first year of eligibility. Harrison and Starr attended the ceremony with Lennon's widow, Yoko Ono, and his two sons, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Lennon" title="Julian Lennon">Julian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sean_Lennon" title="Sean Lennon">Sean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame2009_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame2009-283">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2002753_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2002753-284">[277]</a></sup> McCartney declined to attend, citing unresolved "business differences" that would make him "feel like a complete hypocrite waving and smiling with them at a fake reunion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2002753_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2002753-284">[277]</a></sup> The following year, EMI/Capitol settled a decade-long lawsuit filed by the band over royalties, clearing the way to commercially package previously unreleased material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKozinn1989_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKozinn1989-285">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2002192_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2002192-286">[279]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span></h4>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Live_at_the_BBC_(The_Beatles_album)" title="Live at the BBC (The Beatles album)">Live at the BBC</a></i>, the first official release of unissued Beatles performances in seventeen years, appeared in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a661.E2.80.93663_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a661.E2.80.93663-287">[280]</a></sup> That same year McCartney, Harrison and Starr collaborated on the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_Anthology" title="The Beatles Anthology">Anthology</a></i> project. <i>Anthology</i> was the culmination of work begun in 1970, when Apple Corps director <a href="/wiki/Neil_Aspinall" title="Neil Aspinall">Neil Aspinall</a>, their former road manager and personal assistant, had started to gather material for a documentary with the working title <i>The Long and Winding Road</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a110.E2.80.93111_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a110.E2.80.93111-288">[281]</a></sup> Documenting their history in the band's own words, the <i>Anthology</i> project included the release of several unissued Beatles recordings. McCartney, Harrison and Starr also added new instrumental and vocal parts to two songs recorded as demos by Lennon in the late 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a111.E2.80.93112.2C_428.2C_907.E2.80.93908_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarry2000a111.E2.80.93112.2C_428.2C_907.E2.80.93908-289">[282]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 1995–96, the project yielded a television miniseries, an eight-volume video set, and three two-CD/three-LP box sets featuring artwork by Klaus Voormann. The two songs based on Lennon demos, "<a href="/wiki/Free_as_a_Bird" title="Free as a Bird">Free as a Bird</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Love_(John_Lennon_song)" title="Real Love (John Lennon song)">Real Love</a>", were issued as new Beatles singles.
